   <div class=\"partner-logo-alignment\">\n                <!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <!-- end secondary-header -->\n<!-- Secondary header ends --><div class=\"da1-da2\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle\" id=\"page-content\">\n            <div class=\"da1 ta1 article-head\">\n                <!-- Start Eyebrow block, containing Surtitle and Labels -->\n<div class=\"eyebrow\">\n\t<!-- Start Collection Labels -->\n    <!-- End Collection Labels --> </div>\n<!-- End Eyebrow block -->\n\n<h1 itemprop=\"headline\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-title\">Precise Time Delays from Strongly Gravitationally Lensed Type Ia Supernovae with Chromatically Microlensed Images</h1>\n\n<p class=\"mb-0\">\n\t<span data-authors=\"\">\n\t\t<span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">Daniel A. This includes author emails, affiliations, article published date, doi etc. -->\n\t<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 wd-jnl-art-article-info-wrapper\">\n      \t<div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n        \t<a id=\"wd-article-info-accordion\" href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide article information\">Article information</a>\n      \t</div>\n      \t<div class=\"reveal-content\">\n        \t<div class=\"article-meta\">\n          \t\t<!-- Start Authors --><div class=\"wd-jnl-art-email-addresses\"><div class=\"replica-h4\">Author e-mails</div><p class=\"mb-0\"><a href=\"mailto:dgold@berkeley.edu\" title=\"Send mail to dgold@berkeley.edu\">dgold@berkeley.edu</a></p></div><!-- End Authors --><!-- Start Affiliations --><div class=\"wd-jnl-art-author-affiliations\"><div class=\"replica-h4\">Author affiliations</div><p class=\"mb-05\"><sup>1</sup>  Department of Astronomy, University of California, Berkeley, CA 94720, USA</p><p class=\"mb-05\"><sup>2</sup>  Computational Cosmology Center, Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory, 1 Cyclotron Road, Berkeley, CA 94720, USA</p><p class=\"mb-05\"><sup>3</sup>  Nuclear Science Division, Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory, 1 Cyclotron Road, Berkeley, CA 94720, USA</p><p class=\"mb-05\"><sup>4</sup>  Department of Physics, University of California, Berkeley, CA 94720, USA</p><p class=\"mb-05\"><sup>5</sup>  Institute of Cosmology and Gravitation, University of Portsmouth, Dennis Sciama Building, Burnaby Road, Portsmouth, PO1 3FX, UK</p></div><!-- End Affiliations --><!-- Start ORCIDs --><div class=\"wd-jnl-art-author-orcid-list\"><div class=\"replica-h4\">ORCID iDs</div><p class=\"mb-05\"><span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">Daniel A. <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F855%2F1%2F22\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/855/1/22</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n             <P>Time delays between the multiple images of strongly gravitationally lensed Type Ia supernovae (glSNe\u00a0Ia) have the potential to deliver precise cosmological constraints, but the effects of microlensing on time delay extraction have not been studied in detail. Here we quantify the effect of microlensing on the glSN\u00a0Ia yield of the Large Synoptic Survey Telescope (LSST) and the effect of microlensing on the precision and accuracy of time delays that can be extracted from LSST glSNe\u00a0Ia. Microlensing has a negligible effect on the LSST glSN\u00a0Ia yield, but it can be increased by a factor of ~2 over previous predictions to 930 systems using a novel photometric identification technique based on spectral template fitting. Crucially, the microlensing of glSNe\u00a0Ia is achromatic until three rest-frame weeks after the explosion, making the early-time color curves microlensing-insensitive time delay indicators. By fitting simulated flux and color observations of microlensed glSNe\u00a0Ia with their underlying, unlensed spectral templates, we forecast the distribution of absolute time delay error due to microlensing for LSST, which is unbiased at the sub-percent level and peaked at 1% for color curve observations in the achromatic phase, while for light-curve observations it is comparable to state-of-the-art mass modeling uncertainties (4%). About 70% of LSST glSN\u00a0Ia images should be discovered during the achromatic phase, indicating that microlensing time delay uncertainties can be minimized if prompt multicolor follow-up observations are obtained. Accounting for microlensing, the 1\u20132 day time delay on the recently discovered glSN\u00a0Ia iPTF16geu can be measured to 40% precision, limiting its cosmological utility.</P> </div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/855/1/22&doi=10.3847/1538-4357/aaa975&exportFormat=iopexport_bib&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-bib\" title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/855/1/22&doi=10.3847/1538-4357/aaa975&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-ris\" title=\"Export RIS\">RIS</a>\n\t\t</span>\n\t</p>\n<!-- End Export citation and abstract tools -->\n<!-- Start AAS Related Links -->\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 mt-2 mb-2 wd-jnl-art-article-aas-related-links\">\n  <div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n    <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2018ApJ...855...22G\" target=\"_blank\">NASA ADS Record <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://nedwww.ipac.caltech.edu/cgi-bin/nph-objsearch?refcode=2018ApJ...855...22G&search_type=Search \" target=\"_blank\">NED Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://simbad.u-strasbg.fr/simbad/sim-ref?querymethod=bib&simbo=on&bibcode=2018ApJ...855...22G\" target=\"_blank\">Simbad Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/0004-637X/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaaa975s1\" name=\"apjaaa975s1\">1.\u00a0Introduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Since the discovery of cosmic acceleration (Riess et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib51\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib51\">1998</a>; Perlmutter et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib48\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib48\">1999</a>), <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn1.gif\" alt=\"${\\rm{\\Lambda }}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\rm{\\Lambda }}</script></span></span>CDM has become the observationally favored cosmology, implying that the universe is spatially flat, that it contains cold dark matter and baryons, and that its accelerated expansion is driven by a cosmological constant. Recently, a deviation from <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn2.gif\" alt=\"${\\rm{\\Lambda }}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\rm{\\Lambda }}</script></span></span>CDM was reported by Riess et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib52\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib52\">2016</a>), whose measurement of the Hubble constant <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> using the cosmic distance ladder is in 3.4<em>\u03c3</em> tension with the value inferred from the cosmic microwave background (CMB; Planck Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib49\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib49\">2016</a>), assuming a \u039bCDM cosmology and the standard model of particle physics. Independent measurements of <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> with percent-level accuracy are necessary to determine whether the discrepancy is due to new physics (e.g., a new neutrino species; Riess et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib52\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib52\">2016</a>; Bonvin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib5\">2017</a>) or to systematics.</p><p>Strong gravitational lensing is an independent probe of the cosmological parameters (Oguri et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib45\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib45\">2012</a>; Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib60\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib60\">2013</a>; Collett &amp; Auger <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib11\">2014</a>). Time delays between multiple images of strongly gravitationally lensed variable sources are particularly sensitive to <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>, making them ideal tools to test this discrepancy. They also are sensitive to other parameters of the cosmological model, such as the dark energy equation of state and its evolution with redshift (Linder <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib35\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib35\">2004</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib36\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib36\">2011</a>; Treu &amp; Marshall <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib65\">2016</a>).</p><p>Measuring cosmological parameters to percent level accuracy with strong lens time delays requires three main ingredients (Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib61\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib61\">2017</a>). First, one must measure the time delays (e.g., Tewes et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib63\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib63\">2013</a>; Bonvin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib5\">2017</a>). Second, the lensing potential must be inferred to convert the observed time delays into measurements of the time delay distance (e.g., Wong et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib71\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib71\">2017</a>). This relies on reconstruction of the extended features of a lensed host. Finally, the effect of weak lensing by mass close to the lens and along the line of sight must be included (e.g., Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib62\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib62\">2010</a>; Collett et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib13\">2013</a>; Rusu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib53\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib53\">2017</a>; McCully et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib39\">2017</a>), since lenses are typically found in overdense regions of the universe (Fassnacht et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib15\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib15\">2011</a>).</p><p>To date, time delay cosmography has only been attempted with strongly lensed active galactic nuclei (AGNs; e.g., Vuissoz et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib66\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib66\">2008</a>; Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib60\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib60\">2013</a>; Tewes et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib63\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib63\">2013</a>; Bonvin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib6\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib6\">2016</a>). Lensed AGNs complicate these ingredients, making percent-level constraints on <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> difficult. Because the light curves of AGNs are stochastic and heterogeneous, they typically require years of cadenced monitoring to yield precise time delays (Liao et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib34\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib34\">2015</a>). Inferring the lensing potential by reconstructing the lensed host light is challenging since AGNs typically outshine their host galaxies by several magnitudes. Detecting lensed AGNs requires observations of multiple images introducing a selection function for larger Einstein radii and hence an overdense line of sight (Collett &amp; Cunnington <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib12\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib12\">2016</a>), leading to systematic overestimates of <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>.</p><p>In contrast, the light curves of Type Ia supernovae (SNe\u00a0Ia) are remarkably homogeneous, and strongly lensed SN\u00a0Ia (glSN\u00a0Ia) light curves evolve over weeks, not years, allowing their time delays to be measured with far less observational overhead than those of AGNs. In addition, glSNe\u00a0Ia fade away, allowing a simpler reconstruction of the lensed hosts. glSNe\u00a0Ia can be detected without resolving multiple images (Goobar et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib17\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib17\">2017</a>), simplifying the selection function. Because glSNe\u00a0Ia are standardizable candles, they also have the potential to directly determine the lensing magnification factor <em>\u03bc</em>, which breaks the degeneracy between the lens potential and the Hubble constant (Oguri &amp; Kawano <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib46\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib46\">2003</a>), if the microlensing and macrolensing magnifications can be separated. The well known spectral energy distributions (SEDs) of SNe\u00a0Ia also allow one to correct for extinction along the paths of each SN\u00a0Ia image\u2014another major advantage over AGNs.</p><p>So far, only one glSN\u00a0Ia, iPTF16geu, has been discovered with resolved images (Goobar et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib17\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib17\">2017</a>). However, future surveys, especially the Large Synoptic Survey Telescope (LSST; LSST Science Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib37\">2009</a>), are expected to discover hundreds (Goldstein &amp; Nugent <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib16\">2017</a>). Thus the prospects for discovering a sufficient number of glSNe\u00a0Ia to perform time-delay cosmography in the near future are good.</p><p>However, there is a foreground that threatens this outlook: microlensing. It has long been known that lens galaxy field stars can significantly magnify and demagnify cosmologically distant background AGNs (Chang &amp; Refsdal <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib9\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib9\">1979</a>) and supernovae (Bagherpour et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib2\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib2\">2006</a>; Dobler &amp; Keeton <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib14\">2006</a>). In the microlensing scenario, different macroimages of the same source propagate through different regions of the lens galaxy, passing through distinct lens galaxy star fields. The star fields possess rich networks of caustics that introduce magnification patterns into the source plane. These patterns vary over characteristic angular scales of microarcseconds (hence \"microlensing,\" see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f1\">1</a>), which are typically comparable to the physical sizes of supernovae and AGNs. Thus, as a strongly lensed supernova expands over the source plane, it can experience time- and wavelength-dependent magnifications unique to each lensed image, distorting their light curves and spectra in different ways. These distortions can make it harder to \"match up\" the light curves of multiple images and extract an accurate time delay.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975fn1\">6</a></sup>\n\n</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaa975f1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaa975f1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0Source-plane magnification patterns of 9 of the 78,184 lens galaxy star fields considered in this analysis. Each panel consists of 1000<sup>2</sup> pixels and has a side length of 10 times the Einstein radius of a 1 <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn3.gif\" alt=\"${M}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{\\odot }</script></span></span> deflector projected onto the source plane. The detailed parameters of each map are given in Table <a class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaaa975t1\">1</a>. The size of the exterior shell (<span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn4.gif\" alt=\"$4\\times {10}^{4}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">4\\times {10}^{4}</script></span></span> <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn5.gif\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}</script></span></span>) of the SN\u00a0Ia model W7 at 20 (50) days after explosion is plotted as the interior (exterior) purple circle at the center of each map. Negative (positive) \u0394<em>m</em> indicates magnification (demagnification) over the value from a smooth mass model without microlensing.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaa975f1\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaa975f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaa975f1\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaa975f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Microlensing of lensed variable sources is more than a theoretical exercise: it has been reported in many strongly lensed quasars (e.g., Kayser et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib27\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib27\">1986</a>), and there is also evidence that it affects the images of iPTF16geu (More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib41\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib41\">2017</a>). Dobler &amp; Keeton (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib14\">2006</a>) estimated that microlensing can introduce uncertainties of several days into the features of supernova light curves that can yield time delays. Typical time delays for glSNe\u00a0Ia are a couple of weeks (Goldstein &amp; Nugent <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib16\">2017</a>), translating to a typical fractional time delay uncertainty of ~20%. At this precision, ~400 glSN\u00a0Ia time delays would be required to reach a 1% uncertainty on <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>, assuming no other sources of error, whereas a single glSN\u00a0Ia time delay with 1% precision could accomplish the same goal. Thus, controlling microlensing is of critical importance to the success of time delay cosmography with glSNe\u00a0Ia.</p><p>If, as proposed by Goldstein &amp; Nugent (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib16\">2017</a>), the microlensing magnification affects all wavelengths equally (i.e., if it is \"achromatic\")\u2014then one could use the color curves of glSNe\u00a0Ia instead of the broadband light curves to extract time delays even if the images are affected by microlensing. For example, for a given image, if the <em>B</em>-band is macro- and microlensed as much as the <em>U</em>-band, then in the <em>U</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>B</em> color curve, the micro- and macrolensing magnifications will both cancel out, leaving features common to the color curves of all images that can pin down the time delays to high precision. This would enable color curves of different images to be compared meaningfully, yielding time delays with less uncertainty.</p><p>In this paper, we use detailed radiation transport simulations of a well-understood SN\u00a0Ia model to assess the viability of extracting time delays from the color curves of glSNe\u00a0Ia to circumvent the effects of microlensing. We also perform the first glSN\u00a0Ia yield calculation that takes microlensing into account. The structure of the paper is as follows. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975s2\">2</a>, we describe the radiation transport, glSN\u00a0Ia population, and microlensing models used to synthesize representative microlensed glSN\u00a0Ia SEDs. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975s3\">3</a>, we present the results of our simulations and use them to show that the microlensing of glSNe\u00a0Ia exhibits an achromatic phase at early times. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975s4\">4</a>, we present a novel spectral template-based glSN\u00a0Ia photometric detection technique and use it to forecast the glSN\u00a0Ia yield of LSST. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975s5\">5</a>, we forecast the time delay uncertainty due to microlensing and show that it can be controlled to 1% for typical LSST systems. We conclude in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975s6\">6</a>. Throughout this paper, we assume a Planck Collaboration et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib49\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib49\">2016</a>) cosmology with <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn6.gif\" alt=\"${{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{{\\rm{\\Lambda }}}=0.6925$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{{\\rm{\\Lambda }}}=0.6925</script></span></span>, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn7.gif\" alt=\"${{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{m}=0.3075$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{m}=0.3075</script></span></span>, and <em>h</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.6774.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaaa975s2\" name=\"apjaaa975s2\">2.\u00a0Population, Radiation Transport, and Microlensing Simulations</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In this section, we describe the simulation framework we use to generate a realistic population of glSNe\u00a0Ia. First, the framework realizes a population of unlensed SNe\u00a0Ia and elliptical galaxy lenses using measured redshift distributions. It solves the lens equation for supernovae and lenses close together on the sky, and when a multiply imaged system is produced, it yields image multiplicities, time delays, magnifications, and image positions. For each lensed supernova image, the framework generates a microlensing magnification pattern based on the image properties. A theoretical SN\u00a0Ia spectral time series is convolved with the magnification pattern, giving the lensing amplification of the supernova SED as a function of time and wavelength. This in turn is applied to an empirical SN\u00a0Ia SED template. Realistic LSST light curves are generated from these microlensed spectral templates and fed to a novel detection algorithm.</p><h3 id=\"apjaaa975s2-1\" name=\"apjaaa975s2-1\">2.1.\u00a0The Strongly Lensed Type Ia Supernova Population</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>In the present analysis, we use the same glSN\u00a0Ia population model as that in Goldstein &amp; Nugent (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib16\">2017</a>). We consider only elliptical galaxy lenses and model their mass distribution as a Singular Isothermal Ellipsoid (SIE; Kormann et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib31\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib31\">1994</a>), which has shown excellent agreement with observations (e.g., Koopmans et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib30\">2009</a>). The SIE convergence <em>\u03ba</em> is given by</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn1.gif\" alt=\"Equation (1)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&\\kappa (x,y)=\\displaystyle \\frac{{\\theta }_{E}}{2}\\displaystyle \\frac{\\lambda (e)}{\\sqrt{{(1-e)}^{-1}{x}^{2}+(1-e){y}^{2}}},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn2\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn2.gif\" alt=\"Equation (2)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\theta }_{E}=4\\pi {\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{\\sigma }{c}\\right)}^{2}\\displaystyle \\frac{{D}_{\\mathrm{ls}}}{{D}_{s}}.\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>In the above equations, <em>\u03c3</em> is the velocity dispersion of the lens galaxy, <em>e</em> is its ellipticity, and <em>\u03bb</em>(<em>e</em>) is its so-called \"dynamical normalization,\" a parameter related to three-dimensional shape. Here we make the simplifying assumption that there are an equal number of oblate and prolate galaxies, which Chae (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib7\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib7\">2003</a>) showed implies <em>\u03bb</em>(<em>e</em>)\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a01. As in Oguri et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib44\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib44\">2008</a>), we assume that <em>e</em> follows a truncated normal distribution on the interval [0.0, 0.9], with <em>\u03bc</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.3, <em>\u03c3</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.16.</p><p>We also include external shear to account for the effect of the lens environment (e.g., Kochanek <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib29\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib29\">1991</a>; Keeton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib28\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib28\">1997</a>; Witt &amp; Mao <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib70\">1997</a>). We assume <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn8.gif\" alt=\"${\\mathrm{log}}_{10}{\\gamma }_{\\mathrm{ext}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\mathrm{log}}_{10}{\\gamma }_{\\mathrm{ext}}</script></span></span> follows a normal distribution with mean \u22121.30 and scale 0.2, consistent with the level of external shear expected from ray tracing in <em>N</em>-body simulations (Holder &amp; Schechter <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib20\">2003</a>). The orientation of the external shear is assumed to be random.</p><p>We model the velocity distribution of elliptical galaxies as a modified Schechter function (Sheth et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib58\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib58\">2003</a>):</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn3\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn3.gif\" alt=\"Equation (3)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{dn}\\,=\\,\\phi (\\sigma )d\\sigma ={\\phi }_{* }{\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{\\sigma }{{\\sigma }_{* }}\\right)}^{\\alpha }\\exp \\left[-{\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{\\sigma }{{\\sigma }_{* }}\\right)}^{\\beta }\\right]\\displaystyle \\frac{\\beta }{{\\rm{\\Gamma }}(\\alpha /\\beta )}\\displaystyle \\frac{d\\sigma }{\\sigma },\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where \u0393 is the gamma function, and <em>dn</em> is the differential number of galaxies per unit velocity dispersion per unit comoving volume. We use the parameter values of Choi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib10\">2007</a>) from the Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS; York et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib72\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib72\">2000</a>): (<em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phgr.gif\" alt=\"phgr\" align=\"absmiddle\" /></em><sub>*</sub>, <em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub>, <em>\u03b1</em>, <em>\u03b2</em>)\u00a0=\u00a0(8\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>\u22123</sup> <em>h</em><sup>3</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22123</sup>, 161 <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn9.gif\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}</script></span></span>, 2.32, 2.67). We assume that the mass distribution and velocity function do not evolve with redshift, which is consistent with the results of Chae (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib8\">2007</a>), Oguri et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib44\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib44\">2008</a>), and Bezanson et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib4\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib4\">2011</a>).</p><p>To convert Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975eqn3\">3</a>) into a redshift distribution, we use the definition of the comoving volume element:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn4\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn4.gif\" alt=\"Equation (4)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{{dV}}_{C}={D}_{H}\\displaystyle \\frac{{(1+z)}^{2}{D}_{A}^{2}}{E(z)}\\,{dzd}{\\rm{\\Omega }},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t4\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn10.gif\" alt=\"${D}_{H}=c/{H}_{0}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{D}_{H}=c/{H}_{0}</script></span></span> is the Hubble distance, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn11.gif\" alt=\"$E(z)\\,=\\sqrt{{{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{M}{(1+z)}^{3}+{{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{{\\rm{\\Lambda }}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">E(z)\\,=\\sqrt{{{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{M}{(1+z)}^{3}+{{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{{\\rm{\\Lambda }}}}</script></span></span> in our assumed cosmology, and <em>D</em><sub><em>A</em></sub> is the angular diameter distance. Since <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn12.gif\" alt=\"${dn}\\,=\\,{dN}/{{dV}}_{C}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{dn}\\,=\\,{dN}/{{dV}}_{C}</script></span></span>, for the unnormalized all-sky <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn13.gif\" alt=\"$(d{\\rm{\\Omega }}=4\\pi )$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">(d{\\rm{\\Omega }}=4\\pi )</script></span></span> galaxy distribution, we have</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn5\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn5.gif\" alt=\"Equation (5)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&\\displaystyle \\frac{{dN}}{d\\sigma {dz}}=4\\pi {D}_{H}\\displaystyle \\frac{{(1+z)}^{2}{D}_{A}^{2}}{E(z)}\\phi (\\sigma ).\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t5\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n</p><p>Integrating Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975eqn3\">3</a>) over 0\u00a0\u2264\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0\u2264\u00a01 and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn14.gif\" alt=\"${10}^{1.7}\\,\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}\\,\\leqslant \\sigma \\leqslant {10}^{2.6}\\,\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{10}^{1.7}\\,\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}\\,\\leqslant \\sigma \\leqslant {10}^{2.6}\\,\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}</script></span></span>, we find that there are <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn15.gif\" alt=\"${N}_{\\mathrm{gal}}\\simeq 3.8\\times {10}^{8}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{N}_{\\mathrm{gal}}\\simeq 3.8\\times {10}^{8}</script></span></span> elliptical galaxies, all sky, that can act as strong lenses. This gives the joint probability density function for <em>\u03c3</em> and <em>z</em>:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn6\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn6.gif\" alt=\"Equation (6)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&p(\\sigma ,z)=\\displaystyle \\frac{1}{{N}_{\\mathrm{gal}}}\\displaystyle \\frac{{dN}}{d\\sigma {dz}}.\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t6\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n</p><p>SNe\u00a0Ia exhibit a redshift-dependent volumetric rate and an intrinsic dispersion in rest-frame <em>M</em><sub><em>B</em></sub>. In our model of the SN\u00a0Ia population, we take the redshift-dependent SN\u00a0Ia rate from Sullivan et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib59\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib59\">2000</a>). We assume that the peak rest-frame <em>M</em><sub><em>B</em></sub> is normally distributed with <em>\u03bc</em><sub><em>M</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a0\u221219.3 and <em>\u03c3</em><sub><em>M</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.2. For simplicity, we neglect extinction.</p><p>The lens and source populations are realized in a Monte Carlo simulation. We generate 10<sup>5</sup> lens galaxies with parameters drawn at random from their underlying distributions. For each lens galaxy, an effective lensing area of influence is estimated as a <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn16.gif\" alt=\"${[8{\\theta }_{E,{z}_{s}=\\infty }]}^{2}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{[8{\\theta }_{E,{z}_{s}=\\infty }]}^{2}</script></span></span> box centered on the galaxy.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975fn2\">7</a></sup>\n We simulate 5\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>4</sup> years of SNe\u00a0Ia, randomly distributed across the box, rejecting systems where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn17.gif\" alt=\"${z}_{s}\\lt {z}_{l}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{z}_{s}\\lt {z}_{l}</script></span></span>. For each remaining source, we solve the lens equation using <tt>glafic</tt> (Oguri <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib43\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib43\">2010</a>) to determine the macrolensing magnification, image multiplicity, and time delays. In total, we generated 37,100 multiply imaged systems containing a total of 78,184 images. Since our simulation only covers <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn18.gif\" alt=\"${10}^{5}/{N}_{\\mathrm{gal}}\\approx 0.026 \\% $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{10}^{5}/{N}_{\\mathrm{gal}}\\approx 0.026 \\%</script></span></span> of the sky, this corresponds to a rate of 2675 systems, all sky, per year to <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a02.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaaa975s2-2\" name=\"apjaaa975s2-2\">2.2.\u00a0Microlensing Magnification Patterns</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>For each image, we calculate a source-plane magnification pattern with <tt>microlens</tt> (Wambsganss <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib67\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib67\">1990</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib68\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib68\">1999</a>), an inverse ray-tracing code. In this scheme, stars modeled as point-mass deflectors are realized from a mass function at random locations in a two-dimensional field of the lens galaxy. The size of the pattern is characterized by the Einstein radius <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn19.gif\" alt=\"${\\bar{R}}_{E}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\bar{R}}_{E}</script></span></span> of a deflector of mass <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn20.gif\" alt=\"$\\bar{m}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\bar{m}</script></span></span> projected onto the source plane,</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn7\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn7.gif\" alt=\"Equation (7)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\bar{R}}_{E}=\\sqrt{\\displaystyle \\frac{4G\\bar{m}}{{c}^{2}}\\displaystyle \\frac{{D}_{\\mathrm{ls}}{D}_{s}}{{D}_{l}}},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t7\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>D</em><sub><em>l</em></sub> is the angular diameter distance to the lens, <em>D</em><sub><em>s</em></sub> is the angular diameter distance to the source, and <em>D</em><sub>ls</sub> is the angular diameter distance between the lens and the source. Our magnification patterns are <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn21.gif\" alt=\"$10{\\bar{R}}_{E}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">10{\\bar{R}}_{E}</script></span></span> on a side, which for typical source and lens redshifts (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn22.gif\" alt=\"${z}_{s}=1.2,{z}_{l}=0.6$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{z}_{s}=1.2,{z}_{l}=0.6</script></span></span>) corresponds to an angular scale of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn23.gif\" alt=\"$10{\\bar{R}}_{E}/{D}_{s}\\approx 1.5\\times {10}^{-5}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">10{\\bar{R}}_{E}/{D}_{s}\\approx 1.5\\times {10}^{-5}</script></span></span> arcsec. At the same redshifts, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn24.gif\" alt=\"${\\bar{R}}_{E}\\approx 2.7\\times {10}^{3}\\,\\mathrm{au}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\bar{R}}_{E}\\approx 2.7\\times {10}^{3}\\,\\mathrm{au}</script></span></span>, which is roughly 5 times larger than the extent of the supernova model near peak brightness.</p><p>The magnification patterns are specified by four parameters: (1) the local convergence, <em>\u03ba</em>, (2) the local shear, <em>\u03b3</em>, including the contributions of both the SIE and external potentials, (3) the fraction of the local convergence in stars, <em>f</em><sub>*</sub>,<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975fn3\">8</a></sup>\n and (4) the dynamic range of the stellar mass function, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn25.gif\" alt=\"$q={m}_{\\min }/{m}_{\\max }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">q={m}_{\\min }/{m}_{\\max }</script></span></span>. Supplying these parameters allows <tt>microlens</tt>\u00a0to solve the general microlensing equation, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn26.gif\" alt=\"${\\boldsymbol{\\beta }}={\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}-{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\boldsymbol{\\beta }}={\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}-{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}</script></span></span>, which resolves to</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn8\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn8.gif\" alt=\"Equation (8)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}{\\boldsymbol{\\beta }}=\\left(\\begin{array}{cc}1-\\gamma -{\\kappa }_{c} & 0\\\\ 0 & 1+\\gamma -{\\kappa }_{c}\\end{array}\\right){\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}-\\displaystyle \\sum _{i=1}^{{N}_{* }}\\displaystyle \\frac{{M}_{i}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}-{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{i})}{{({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}-{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{i})}^{2}},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t8\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn27.gif\" alt=\"${\\kappa }_{c}=(1-{f}_{* })\\kappa $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\kappa }_{c}=(1-{f}_{* })\\kappa</script></span></span> is the local convergence in continuously distributed matter, the two-dimensional vector <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn28.gif\" alt=\"${\\boldsymbol{\\beta }}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\boldsymbol{\\beta }}</script></span></span> is the angular position of the source in the absence of lensing, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn29.gif\" alt=\"${\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}</script></span></span> is the angular position of the observed macroimage, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn30.gif\" alt=\"${{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{i}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{i}</script></span></span> is the angular position of the <em>i</em>'th star, and <em>N</em><sub>*</sub> is the number of stars in the field, determined from the local convergence in stars <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn31.gif\" alt=\"${\\kappa }_{* }={f}_{* }\\kappa $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\kappa }_{* }={f}_{* }\\kappa</script></span></span> using the procedure of Schneider &amp; Weiss (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib57\">1987</a>).</p><p>We use a Salpeter (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib55\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib55\">1955</a>) mass function, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn32.gif\" alt=\"${dn}/{dm}\\propto {m}^{-2.35}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{dn}/{dm}\\propto {m}^{-2.35}</script></span></span>, to model the population of stars in our microlensing calculations. As we will show in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975s3\">3</a>, this choice has no effect on our results as the achromaticity of glSN\u00a0Ia microlensing is driven entirely by the color evolution of SNe\u00a0Ia and not by the properties of the microlensing magnification patterns. Following Dobler &amp; Keeton (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib14\">2006</a>), we take the mean mass <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn33.gif\" alt=\"$\\bar{m}=1\\,{M}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\bar{m}=1\\,{M}_{\\odot }</script></span></span>. The microlensing parameters <em>\u03ba</em> and <em>\u03b3</em> are determined by evaluating the SIE and external shear lensing potentials at the location of each image. <em>f</em><sub>*</sub> is estimated following the method of Dobler &amp; Keeton (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib14\">2006</a>), assuming a de Vaucouleurs stellar profile normalized so that the maximum <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn34.gif\" alt=\"${f}_{* }=1$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{f}_{* }=1</script></span></span>. For each image, the dynamic range parameter <em>q</em> is sampled uniformly at random from (0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0), appropriate for the old stellar populations in elliptical galaxies. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f1\">1</a> shows a random selection of nine of the maps, highlighting their morphological diversity. The detailed parameters of each map are given in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaaa975t1\">1</a>. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f2\">2</a> shows two-dimensional projections of the joint distributions of the macrolensing parameters <em>\u03c3</em>, <em>e</em>, and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn35.gif\" alt=\"${\\gamma }_{\\mathrm{ext}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\gamma }_{\\mathrm{ext}}</script></span></span>, the microlensing parameters <em>\u03ba</em>, <em>\u03b3</em>, and <em>f</em><sub>*</sub>, and the time- and wavelength-averaged microlensing magnification <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn36.gif\" local shear. <em>q</em>: mass ratio <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn55.gif\" alt=\"${m}_{\\min }/{m}_{\\max }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{m}_{\\min }/{m}_{\\max }</script></span></span> of the stellar mass function. <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" \\rangle</script></span></span>: magnification of the field from a smooth mass model without microlensing.</p>\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaa975f2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaa975f2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div image macrolensing and microlensing parameters. The input distributions (blue) represent all 78,184 simulated lensed images from Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975s2-1\">2.1</a>, and the detected distributions (red) represent only the images of the glSNe\u00a0Ia detected in Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975s4-2\">4.2</a>. The bimodal input distributions of <em>f</em><sub>*</sub>, <em>\u03b3</em>, and <em>\u03ba</em> represent the amplified <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn37.gif\" alt=\"$({f}_{* }\\approx 0.4)$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">({f}_{* }\\approx 0.4)</script></span></span> and overfocussed <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn38.gif\" alt=\"$({f}_{* }\\approx 1)$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">({f}_{* }\\approx 1)</script></span></span> images produced by SIE lenses. Joint contours show 1<em>\u03c3</em> and 2<em>\u03c3</em>. Marginal shaded regions show 1<em>\u03c3</em>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaa975f2\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaa975f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaa975f2\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaa975f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjaaa975s2-3\" name=\"apjaaa975s2-3\">2.3.\u00a0Supernova Modeling</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We use the well-understood, spherically symmetric<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975fn4\">9</a></sup>\n SN\u00a0Ia atmosphere model W7 (Nomoto et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib42\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib42\">1984</a>) to estimate the time- and wavelength-dependent magnification of glSNe\u00a0Ia due to microlensing. This radiation transport model is the result of a one-dimensional explosion simulation in which a Chandrasekhar-mass carbon\u2013oxygen white dwarf undergoes a deflagration. The explosion of the white dwarf completely unbinds the star and deposits the energy liberated by nuclear burning into the ejected mass. The deposited energy controls the velocity distribution of the ejecta and its density profile, which is assumed to reach homology seconds after the explosion. We use the time-dependent, Monte Carlo radiation transport code <tt>SEDONA</tt> (Kasen et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib25\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib25\">2006</a>) to calculate the spectral time series of the model. Details of our <tt>SEDONA</tt> simulations appear in Appendix <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975app1\">A</a>.</p><p>The observed spectrum <em>F</em><sub><em>\u03bb</em></sub> of the model at wavelength <em>\u03bb</em> and time <em>t</em> is obtained by convolving its time-evolving specific intensity with the lensing amplification pattern over a plane normal to the observer's line of sight. Since the model is spherical, this integral takes the following form:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn9\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn9.gif\" alt=\"Equation (9)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{F}_{\\lambda }(\\lambda ,t)={D}_{L}^{-2}{\\int }_{0}^{2\\pi }\\,{\\int }_{0}^{{P}_{m}}{I}_{\\lambda }(P,\\phi ,\\lambda ,t)\\mu (P,\\phi )P\\,{dP}\\,d\\phi ,\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t9\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phgr.gif\" alt=\"phgr\" align=\"absmiddle\" /></em> and <em>P</em> are azimuthal and impact parameter coordinates on the plane, <em>I</em><sub><em>\u03bb</em></sub> is the specific intensity of the model, <em>\u03bc</em> is the lensing amplification,<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975fn5\">10</a></sup>\n <em>D</em><sub><em>L</em></sub> is the luminosity distance to the supernova, and <em>P</em><sub><em>m</em></sub> is the maximum impact parameter of the model. For a derivation of Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975eqn9\">9</a>), see Appendix <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975app2\">B</a>. The time- and wavelength-dependent magnification of a given magnification pattern is obtained by dividing <em>F</em><sub><em>\u03bb</em></sub> by the unlensed spectrum of the model (Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975eqn9\">9</a>) with <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0=\u00a01).</p><p>We interpolate each magnification pattern bilinearly and convolve it with the redshifted<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975fn6\">11</a></sup>\n specific intensities of the supernova model. The redshift configurations control the projected size of the supernova on the magnification pattern and thus the magnification experienced by each differential element of the projected supernova atmosphere. They also control <em>\u03b8</em><sub><em>E</em></sub> and thus <em>\u03ba</em>, <em>\u03b3</em>, and <em>f</em><sub>*</sub> at the location of the image. We always place the supernova model at the center of the magnification pattern. We model the homologous expansion of the supernova behind the magnification pattern (i.e., the projected size of the supernova on the magnification pattern changes with time), but not relative motion between the supernova and the lens galaxy star field. In general, supernova atmospheres both expand and move with respect to the lens galaxy, but the characteristic expansion velocity of the atmosphere (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn40.gif\" alt=\"$\\sim {10}^{4}\\,\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\sim {10}^{4}\\,\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}</script></span></span>) is much larger than the characteristic relative velocity between the lens galaxy and the supernova (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn41.gif\" alt=\"$\\sim {10}^{2}\\,\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\sim {10}^{2}\\,\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}</script></span></span>), so here we model only the effects of expansion.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaaa975s3\" name=\"apjaaa975s3\">3.\u00a0Two Phases of Type Ia Supernova Microlensing</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Example spectra and difference light curves of our microlensed SN\u00a0Ia atmosphere appear in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f3\">3</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f4\">4</a>, respectively; confidence regions of all <em>U</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>B</em>, <em>B</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>V</em>, <em>V</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>R</em>, and <em>R</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>I</em> color curves produced by our simulation appear in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f5\">5</a>. The difference light curves give the microlensing amplification in magnitudes,</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn10\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn10.gif\" alt=\"Equation (10)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\rm{\\Delta }}M(t)=-2.5{\\mathrm{log}}_{10}\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{L(t)}{{\\mu }_{0}U(t)}\\right),\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t10\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>U</em>(<em>t</em>) and <em>L</em>(<em>t</em>) are the unlensed and observed fluxes of the supernova, respectively, and <em>\u03bc</em><sub>0</sub> is the magnification in the absence of microlensing (i.e., if there were only macrolensing due to the lens galaxy). In the absence of microlensing, \u0394<em>M</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaa975f3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaa975f3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Rest-frame spectra of model W7 (Nomoto et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib42\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib42\">1984</a>) computed with <tt>SEDONA</tt> near peak brightness and well into the onset of the infrared secondary maximum. The solid curve is unlensed and the dashed curve is lensed by star field (h) from Figure <a href=\"#apjaaa975f1\">1</a>. Near peak brightness, microlensing does not have a large effect on the shape of the spectrum or the relative strengths of its features. During the chromatic phase, lensing-induced continuum shifts and spectral line distortions are visible in the ratios of the spectra. Such chromatic distortions can affect the colors of the supernova.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaa975f3\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaa975f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaa975f3\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaa975f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaa975f4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaa975f4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f4_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f4_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0Four randomly chosen examples of broadband rest-frame difference light curves of model W7 computed by <tt>SEDONA</tt>. Each set of difference light curves has two distinct phases: an \"achromatic\" phase in which \u0394<em>M</em> is the same in all bands to within a few millimag, and a \"chromatic\" phase in which \u0394<em>M</em> can vary significantly from band to band.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaa975f4\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaa975f4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaa975f4\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaa975f4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaa975f5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaa975f5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f5_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f5_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0Rest-frame microlensed color curves of model W7. The intervals containing 68%, 95%, and 99% of the 78,184 microlensed color curves described in Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975s3\">3</a> are plotted as progressively less opaque shaded regions. The color curves of the unlensed model are indistinguishable from the 68% confidence regions of the microlensed models.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaa975f5\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaa975f5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaa975f5\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaa975f5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Each of these figures demonstrates that glSN\u00a0Ia microlensing has two phases, an \"achromatic\" phase, in which the microlensing magnification is the same at all wavelengths to a few millimag, followed by a \"chromatic\" phase, in which the microlensing magnification varies strongly (and unpredictably) with wavelength. The difference light curves show that the achromatic phase lasts roughly three rest-frame weeks after the explosion, transitioning to a chromatic phase between the time of peak brightness and the onset of the infrared secondary maximum.
