Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f6\">6</a> shows the specific intensity profile <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn42.gif\" alt=\"${I}_{\\lambda }(v)$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{I}_{\\lambda }(v)</script></span></span> of our unlensed model at 20 and 40 days after explosion, where <em>v</em> is the velocity of the shell, equivalent to a radial variable (i.e., <em>P</em>).<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975fn7\">12</a></sup>\n The left panel of the figure shows that near peak (20 days after explosion), the ratio <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn43.gif\" alt=\"${I}_{{X}_{1}}(v)/{I}_{{X}_{2}}(v)$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{I}_{{X}_{1}}(v)/{I}_{{X}_{2}}(v)</script></span></span>, where <em>X</em><sub>1</sub> and <em>X</em><sub>2</sub> are any two bands, is roughly constant over all <em>v</em>. Thus the supernova near peak has a specific intensity profile that is independent of <em>v</em> up to an overall normalization factor. As a result, any magnification pattern <em>\u03bc</em>(<em>P</em>, <em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phgr.gif\" alt=\"phgr\" align=\"absmiddle\" /></em>) will not change its color.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaa975f6\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaa975f6\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f6_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 6.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 6.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f6_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 6.</strong>\u00a0Normalized projected specific intensity profiles of model W7. Near peak, the specific intensity profiles in <em>UBVRI</em> are similar, so microlensing is achromatic. At day 40, UV line blanketing and the \"fluorescent shell\" (in which Fe <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span> \u2192 Fe <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> recombination occurs) causes different bands to have different specific intensity profiles. As a result, microlensing is chromatic at this stage.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaa975f6\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaa975f6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaa975f6\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaa975f6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>However, after peak, the supernova expands and cools enough for some part of the atmosphere to reach a temperature of 7000 K. Kasen (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib24\">2006</a>) and Kasen &amp; Woosley (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib26\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib26\">2007</a>) note that this is the temperature at which Fe <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span> recombines to Fe <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>, which presents a significantly higher opacity to blue and ultraviolet radiation than Fe <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>. This line blanketing has the effect of enabling one to see redder emission from deeper in the supernova, while emission in the blue and the UV is pushed to larger radii. Additionally, a \"fluorescent shell\" of iron recombination, which causes a peak in the redder bands in the specific intensity profile of the supernova, develops near the onset of the secondary maximum. This shell is clearly visible in the righthand panel of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f6\">6</a>. These two effects, line blanketing and a fluorescent shell, make the supernova's specific intensity ratio no longer spatially constant. As a result, the supernova atmosphere becomes susceptible to chromatic fluctuations.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaaa975s4\" name=\"apjaaa975s4\">4.\u00a0The Effect of Microlensing on LSST Lensed Type Ia Supernova Yields</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Previous estimates of glSN\u00a0Ia yields, including those of Oguri &amp; Marshall (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib47\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib47\">2010</a>), Quimby et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib50\">2014</a>), and Goldstein &amp; Nugent (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib16\">2017</a>), modeled only the effects of macrolensing. In this section, we present a novel method of identifying glSNe\u00a0Ia photometrically based on spectral template fitting. We apply this technique to our simulated micro- and macrolensed glSN\u00a0Ia light curves to present a new estimate of glSN\u00a0Ia yields for LSST.</p><h3 id=\"apjaaa975s4-1\" name=\"apjaaa975s4-1\">4.1.\u00a0Efficient Identification of Lensed Type Ia Supernovae with Spectral Template Fitting</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Our detection strategy rests on three observational facts. First, normal SNe\u00a0Ia are the brightest type of supernovae that have ever been observed to occur in elliptical galaxies (Maoz et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib38\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib38\">2014</a>). Second, the absolute magnitudes of normal SNe\u00a0Ia in elliptical galaxies are remarkably homogenous, even without correcting for their colors or light-curve shapes <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn44.gif\" alt=\"$({\\sigma }_{M}\\sim 0.4\\ \\mathrm{mag})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">({\\sigma }_{M}\\sim 0.4\\ \\mathrm{mag})</script></span></span>, with a component of the population being underluminous (Li et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib33\">2011</a>). Finally, due to the sharp 4000 \u00c5 break in their spectra, elliptical galaxies tend to provide accurate photometric redshifts from large-scale multicolor galaxy surveys such as SDSS.</p><p>A high-cadence, wide-field imaging survey can leverage these facts to systematically search for glSNe\u00a0Ia in the following way. First, by spatially cross-matching its list of supernova candidates with a catalog of elliptical galaxies for which secure photometric redshifts have been obtained, supernovae that appear to be hosted by elliptical galaxies can be identified. The hypothesis that one of these supernovae actually resides in its apparent host can be tested by fitting its broadband light curves with an SN\u00a0Ia spectral template (as SNe\u00a0Ia are the only types of supernovae that occur in ellipticals) fixed to the photometric redshift of the galaxy and constrained to obey <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn45.gif\" alt=\"$-18.5\\gt {M}_{B}\\gt -20$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">-18.5\\gt {M}_{B}\\gt -20</script></span></span>, a liberal absolute magnitude range for SNe\u00a0Ia, assuming a fiducial cosmology. If the transient is a lensed supernova at higher redshift, then the spectral template fit will fail catastrophically, as the supernova light curves will be strongly inconsistent with the redshift and brightness implied by the lens galaxy.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaaa975s4-2\" name=\"apjaaa975s4-2\">4.2.\u00a0Monte Carlo Simulation</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We use SALT2 (Guy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib18\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib18\">2007</a>), a parametrized SN\u00a0Ia spectral template that is the <em>de facto</em> standard tool to place SNe\u00a0Ia on the Hubble diagram, to test this method. The template possesses four parameters: <em>t</em><sub>0</sub>, <em>x</em><sub>0</sub>, <em>x</em><sub>1</sub>, and <em>c</em>, encoding a reference time, an overall SED normalization, a supernova \"stretch,\" and a color-law coefficient, respectively. The flux of the template is given by</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn11\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn11.gif\" alt=\"Equation (11)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{F}_{\\lambda }(\\lambda ,t)={x}_{0}[{M}_{0}(\\lambda ,t)+{x}_{1}{M}_{1}(\\lambda ,t)]\\exp [c\\,\\mathrm{CL}(\\lambda )],\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t11\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>M</em><sub>0</sub> and <em>M</em><sub>1</sub> are eigenspectra derived from a training sample of measured SN\u00a0Ia spectra and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn46.gif\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{CL}(\\lambda )$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\mathrm{CL}(\\lambda )</script></span></span> is the average color-correction law of the sample (see Guy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib18\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib18\">2007</a>, for details). The template aims to model the mean evolution of the SED sequence of SNe\u00a0Ia and its variation with a few dominant components, including a time independent variation with color, whether it is intrinsic or due to extinction by dust in the host galaxy (or both).</p><p>We consider an LSST lensed supernova search in which the photometry is performed with a PSF that is artifically enlarged to blend the multiple images together into a single source. We randomly assign each of the 37,100 simulated glSNe\u00a0Ia from Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975s2-1\">2.1</a> an LSST field from the nominal observing strategy (<tt>minion</tt>_<tt>1016</tt>; LSST Science Collaborations 2018, in preparation).<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975fn8\">13</a></sup>\n We compute the phase- and wavelength- dependent magnification <em>\u03bc</em>(<em>\u03bb</em>, <em>t</em>) of each lensed image by placing its corresponding microlensed W7 SED into the rest frame, then dividing each by the unlensed spectral sequence of the model. We then generate the rest-frame spectral model for the image <em>F</em>(<em>\u03bb</em>, <em>t</em>) according to</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn12\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn12.gif\" alt=\"Equation (12)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&F(\\lambda ,t)=\\mu (\\lambda ,t)H(\\lambda ,t),\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t12\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>H</em>(<em>\u03bb</em>, <em>t</em>) is the Hsiao et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib21\">2007</a>) SN\u00a0Ia spectral template. We employ a warped Hsiao template rather than the microlensed W7 SEDs to mitigate uncertainties in the radiation transport.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975fn9\">14</a></sup>\n We then place the templates of each image at their correct redshifts, and we rescale and time-shift them to account for the macrolensing and time delays. Finally, we draw a random time for the system (arbitrarily chosen to be the observer-frame time of rest-frame <em>B</em>-band maximum of the first image) from the 11 year period spanning 6 months before the beginning of the survey until 6 months following the end of the survey.</p><p>We realize broadband photometry of each blended glSN\u00a0Ia (summing the flux of each microlensed image) using the sky brightnesses, FWHMs, exposure times, observation times, and limiting magnitudes of the assigned field provided by <tt>minion</tt>_<tt>1016</tt>, assuming the total area covered by the survey is 25,000 deg<sup>2</sup>. Starting from the first observation of the SN\u00a0Ia, we fit the light curve with SALT2, fixed to the redshift of the lens galaxy (assumed to be known either as a photometric or spectroscopic redshift) and fixed to obey <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn47.gif\" alt=\"$-18.5\\gt {M}_{B}\\gt -20$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">-18.5\\gt {M}_{B}\\gt -20</script></span></span> at that redshift (effectively a constraint on <em>x</em><sub>0</sub>). Additionally, we enforce bounds of [\u22120.2, 0.2] on <em>c</em> and [\u22121, 1] on <em>x</em><sub>1</sub>, values characteristic of normal SNe\u00a0Ia\u00a0(Scalzo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib56\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib56\">2014</a>). We use the CERN minimization routine <tt>MIGRAD</tt> (James &amp; Roos <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib22\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib22\">1975</a>) to fit the data. If the light curve has at least one data point that is at least 5<em>\u03c3</em> discrepant from the best fit and at least four data points with S/N\u00a0\u2265\u00a05, then the object is marked \"detected.\" If not, then the next observation is added and the process is repeated until the object is detected or all observations are added, resulting in a nondetection.</p><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f7\">7</a> shows an example of this procedure being used to detect one of our simulated glSNe\u00a0Ia at <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a01.91. The red data points show the \"current\" light curve, and the red line shows the best-fit model, fixed to <em>z</em><sub><em>l</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.96. Although the model fits the data well in the bluer bands, the high redshift of the source makes the data much brighter in the infrared than the model expects given the redshift of the lens. Thus the object is detected shortly after peak due to 10<em>\u03c3</em> discrepant points in <em>y</em>-band.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaa975f7\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaa975f7\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f7_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 7.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 7.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f7_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 7.</strong>\u00a0Detecting a <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn48.gif\" alt=\"${z}_{s}=1.91,{z}_{l}=0.96$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{z}_{s}=1.91,{z}_{l}=0.96</script></span></span> LSST glSN\u00a0Ia with SALT2. The red data points show the \"current\" light curve, and the red line shows the best-fit SALT2 model fixed to the photometric redshift of the lens galaxy. The gray points show future observations that are not included in this iteration of the fit. The black vertical line in the residual plots shows the date when the supernova is detected. Although the model fits the data well in the bluer bands, the high redshift of the source makes the data much brighter in the infrared than the model expects given the redshift of the lens. Thus the object is detected shortly after peak due to 10<em>\u03c3</em> discrepant points in <em>y</em>-band.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaa975f7\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaa975f7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaa975f7\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaa975f7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjaaa975s4-3\" name=\"apjaaa975s4-3\">4.3.\u00a0Yields</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Our spectral template fitting approach to glSN\u00a0Ia identification delivers almost twice as many LSST glSNe\u00a0Ia than the method of Goldstein &amp; Nugent (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib16\">2017</a>). In total, LSST should find ~925 microlensed glSNe\u00a0Ia with the new method over the duration of its 10 year survey. This is almost identical to the case with no microlensing, which would yield 935 glSNe\u00a0Ia over the same period, with a nearly identical redshift distribution (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f9\">9</a>). This represents a major increase in the expected glSN\u00a0Ia yield for LSST, comparable to the number of expected lensed quasars (Oguri &amp; Marshall <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib47\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib47\">2010</a>).</p><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f8\">8</a> shows the rest-frame phase distribution of discovered microlensed glSN\u00a0Ia images (a phase of 0 corresponds to peak brightness in <em>B</em>). The 68% confidence interval of the image phase distribution is <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn49.gif\" alt=\"$-{1.01}_{-10.77}^{+10.24}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">-{1.01}_{-10.77}^{+10.24}</script></span></span>, so about half of the images should be discovered before peak brightness. 73% of the images and 64% of the image pairs should be discovered during the achromatic phase.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaa975f8\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaa975f8\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f8_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 8.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 8.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f8_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 8.</strong>\u00a0Phases of detected LSN images when they are discovered. Phases are given relative to each image, not to the peak of the total flux of the multiple blended images of SN. 73% of the images and 64% of the image pairs are discovered during the achromatic phase.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaa975f8\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaa975f8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaa975f8\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaa975f8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaaa975s5\" name=\"apjaaa975s5\">5.\u00a0The Effect of Microlensing on Lensed Type Ia Supernova Time Delays</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975s3\">3</a>, we showed that microlensing introduces time- and wavelength-dependent fluctuations into the light curves of SNe\u00a0Ia. In this section, we quantify the effect of these fluctuations on the time delays that can be extracted from simulated photometric observations of typical LSST glSNe\u00a0Ia, using as input the results of Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975s4-3\">4.3</a>. We demonstrate that microlensing can introduce time delay uncertainties of ~4% into the light curves of typical LSST glSNe\u00a0Ia, but that this number decreases to ~1% when achromatic-phase color curves of the same supernovae are used instead.</p><h3 id=\"apjaaa975s5-1\" name=\"apjaaa975s5-1\">5.1.\u00a0Monte Carlo Simulations of Microlensing Time Delay Uncertainty</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Since many of the glSNe\u00a0Ia that will be discovered by LSST will require higher-spatial resolution follow-up observations to extract time delays, either with ground-based adaptive optics or space-based imaging, we simulate \"time-delay observations\" with the Wide Field Camera 3 (WFC3) on the <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> (<em>HST</em>). The redshift distribution in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f9\">9</a> implies that most LSST glSNe\u00a0Ia will be brightest in the IR, so we use the F814W, F125W, and F160W filters (roughly <em>I</em>, <em>J</em>, and <em>H</em> bands, respectively) on WFC3 for this simulation.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaa975f9\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaa975f9\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f9_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 9.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 9.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f9_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 9.</strong>\u00a0Source and lens redshift distributions of the glSNe\u00a0Ia with at least four data points with S/N\u00a0&gt;\u00a05 detected with (blue) and without microlensing (red). The joint contours show 1<em>\u03c3</em> and 2<em>\u03c3</em>. The marginal shaded regions show 1<em>\u03c3</em>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaa975f9\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaa975f9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaa975f9\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaa975f9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>For each of the \"detected\" microlensed glSN\u00a0Ia systems from Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975s4-3\">4.3</a>, we realize 45 photometric observations of each image in F814W, F125W, and F160W with infinite signal-to-noise, with a uniform temporal spacing, spanning the light curve. The spectral template for each image is the same as that used in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975s4\">4</a> (see Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975eqn12\">12</a>)). Using <tt>MIGRAD</tt>, we fit the realized light curves of each pair of images using the redshifted, unlensed spectral template <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn50.gif\" alt=\"$H(\\lambda ,t)$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">H(\\lambda ,t)</script></span></span>. For each fit, we estimate the fitted time delay as</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn13\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn13.gif\" alt=\"Equation (13)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\rm{\\Delta }}t={t}_{\\mathrm{0,2}}-{t}_{\\mathrm{0,1}},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t13\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn51.gif\" alt=\"${t}_{\\mathrm{0,2}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{t}_{\\mathrm{0,2}}</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn52.gif\" alt=\"${t}_{\\mathrm{0,1}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{t}_{\\mathrm{0,1}}</script></span></span> are the fitted reference times of the second and first images, respectively. We then measure the error on the fitted time delay as</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn14\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn14.gif\" alt=\"Equation (14)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&\\epsilon =\\left|\\displaystyle \\frac{{\\rm{\\Delta }}t-{\\rm{\\Delta }}t^{\\prime} }{{\\rm{\\Delta }}t^{\\prime} }\\right|,\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t14\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn53.gif\" alt=\"${\\rm{\\Delta }}t^{\\prime} $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\rm{\\Delta }}t^{\\prime}</script></span></span> is the true time delay of the pair of images.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975fn10\">15</a></sup>\n\n</p><p>Next, we prune the photometric observations to the achromatic phase, masking all observations more than five rest-frame days from the date of <em>B</em>-band maximum. We then synthesize F814W\u00a0\u2212\u00a0F125W, F125W\u00a0\u2212\u00a0F160W, and F814W\u00a0\u2212\u00a0F160W color curves from the photometry.</p><p>We apply the same fitting procedure to the color curves and estimate the fitted time delays and uncertainties using Equations (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975eqn13\">13</a>) and (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975eqn14\">14</a>). Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f10\">10</a> shows an example of the procedure being applied to the light curves and achromatic-phase color curves of two images of a supernova. While the light curve data show microlensing-induced offsets near peak brightness and the secondary maximum that produce a large time delay error of ~10%, the residuals show that these offsets are nearly the same in all bands during the achromatic phase, and thus the fits to the color curve have errors &lt;1%.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaa975f10\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaa975f10\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f10_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 10.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 10.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f10_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 10.</strong>\u00a0Fitting infinite S/N light curves and color curves (in the achromatic phase) of two images of a microlensed supernova with the unlensed Hsiao template to estimate time delay error. Microlensing produces visible offsets in the features of the light curves, but the residuals show that during the achromatic phase (until a few weeks after peak brightness) the offsets are achromatic. Thus when the color curves are fit in the achromatic phase, the uncertainty on the time delay is more than an order of magnitude smaller.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaa975f10\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaa975f10\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaa975f10\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaa975f10\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f11\">11</a> shows the joint distribution of time delays and microlensing-induced time delay uncertainties for all detected pairs of images in our simulation. We find that the median time delay of detected pairs of images is ~10 days, and that the median microlensing-induced time delay uncertainty using light curve fits is 4%, comparable to the current uncertainties on mass modeling. However, this number drops down to 1% (~2.5 hours on a 10 day time delay) when achromatic-phase color curves are used instead of light curves.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaa975f11\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaa975f11\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f11_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 11.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 11.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f11_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 11.</strong>\u00a0Joint distribution of the true time delay and the microlensing time delay uncertainty as a percentage of the true time delay for all discovered pairs of glSN\u00a0Ia images. The median time delay uncertainty is 4% for light curves, but just 1% for color curve measurements in the achromatic phase. The median time delay that LSST will detect is 10 days. Contours show 1<em>\u03c3</em> and 2<em>\u03c3</em>. Marginal shaded regions show 1<em>\u03c3</em>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaa975f11\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaa975f11\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaa975f11\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaa975f11\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f12\">12</a> shows the systematic microlensing bias on time delays from fitting light curves and color curves in the achromatic phase. The achromatic phase color curve fits are consistent with zero bias down to \u0394<em>t</em>\u00a0=\u00a01 day, while the light curve fits are consistent with zero bias down to \u0394<em>t</em>\u00a0=\u00a0~\u00a0a few days. This result indicates that time delay bias from microlensing will not be a major systematic for cosmography with glSNe\u00a0Ia.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaa975f12\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaa975f12\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f12_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 12.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 12.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f12_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 12.</strong>\u00a0Bias as a function of true time delay for all discovered microlensed image pairs, fitting color curves in the achromatic phase (top panel) and entire light curves (bottom panel). Blue points show individual fits; red points show bin averages.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaa975f12\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaa975f12\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaa975f12\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaa975f12\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjaaa975s5-2\" name=\"apjaaa975s5-2\">5.2.\u00a0iPTF16geu</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>iPTF16geu is the only glSN\u00a0Ia with resolved images that has been discovered to date. Here we consider its potential time delay precision and cosmological impact. Before it faded, multiwavelength follow-up observations of the event were obtained with a variable cadence using the Washington <em>C</em>, <em>g</em>, <em>r</em>, wide <em>I</em>, <em>Z</em>, <em>J</em>, and <em>H</em> bands of WFC3 on <em>HST</em> (DD 14862, PI: Goobar). The bluer bands were only observed sporadically, but the redder bands were observed with a roughly 4-day cadence.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975fn11\">16</a></sup>\n Unfortunately, the observations were obtained well into the chromatic phase, and thus the light curves and color curves exhibit significant microlensing uncertainties. Final photometry of the event has not yet been produced, as not enough time has passed to take final reference images. The current best estimate of the time delay on this system is 35 hours (Goobar et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib17\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib17\">2017</a>). Based on Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f11\">11</a>, we estimate the time delay uncertainty due to microlensing from this event to be ~40%. If the event were discovered earlier so that color curves during the achromatic phase could be constructed, then the microlensing time delay uncertainty would drop to ~10%.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaaa975s6\" name=\"apjaaa975s6\">6.\u00a0Conclusion</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In this article, we assessed the impact of microlensing on the yields and time delay precisions of LSST glSNe\u00a0Ia. We presented microlensed broadband difference light curves and color curves of the well-understood SN\u00a0Ia ejecta model W7 for 78,184 microlensing magnification patterns drawn from a realistic population model of glSN\u00a0Ia images. We found that until shortly after peak brightness, the microlensing of SNe\u00a0Ia is achromatic, and thus time delays from early-time color curves are less sensitive to microlensing than time delays from light curves. We interpreted the achromaticity of microlensing before the onset of the secondary maximum as being due to UV line blanketing and the emergence of a fluorescent shell of Fe <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span> \u2192 <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> recombination that alters the specific intensity profile of the ejecta as suggested by Kasen (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib24\">2006</a>) and Kasen &amp; Woosley (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib26\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib26\">2007</a>). We found that microlensing does not have a significant impact on glSN\u00a0Ia yields, but that they can be increased by a factor of ~2 over the predictions of Goldstein &amp; Nugent (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib16\">2017</a>) using a novel photometric detection techinque. </p><p>Our <tt>SEDONA</tt> calculation of W7 represents the most detailed SN\u00a0Ia spectrum synthesis calculation that has been used to investigate SN\u00a0Ia microlensing to date, but it is inherently one-dimensional and makes a number of physical approximations for computational expediency. It does not perfectly reproduce the observed colors of SNe\u00a0Ia, especially at UV wavelengths, where line blanketing is strong and small differences in the underlying model can have pronounced effects. Additionally, W7 is just one SN\u00a0Ia model, and there is diversity in the SN\u00a0Ia population. However, we expect that this model captures the key physical behavior that leads to chromatic effects, and so we do not expect the results to change significantly with different one-dimensional models. Although there is evidence from spectropolarimetry that global asymmetry in SNe\u00a0Ia is very small (Wang &amp; Wheeler <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib69\">2008</a>), asymmetric explosion scenarios have not been ruled out by observations. In the future, it will be useful to assess whether asymmetric, multi-dimensional supernova models confirm the two phases of glSN\u00a0Ia microlensing identified in this work, or whether viewing angle effects become important.</p><p>Despite the complication of microlensing, time-delays can be robustly measured to sub-percent precision for many glSNe\u00a0Ia. By photometrically detecting the first image of a strongly lensed core-collapse supernova before light from the other images arrives, one can use the sharp shock-breakout light curve as a time delay indicator with precision <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn57.gif\" alt=\"$\\sim (30\\,\\min )(1+{z}_{s})/{\\rm{\\Delta }}t$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\sim (30\\,\\min )(1+{z}_{s})/{\\rm{\\Delta }}t</script></span></span>. Such precision is difficult to achieve with lensed AGNs (Tewes et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib63\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib63\">2013</a>; Bonvin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib5\">2017</a>; Tie &amp; Kochanek <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib64\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib64\">2018</a>), which also require a significantly longer observing campaign (Liao et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib34\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib34\">2015</a>). This result provides a straightforward first step to inferring cosmological parameters with glSNe\u00a0Ia. The latter steps of inferring the lens potential, and the effect of line-of-sight structures have already been implemented for lensed AGNs (e.g., Bonvin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib5\">2017</a>; Rusu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib53\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib53\">2017</a>; Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib61\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib61\">2017</a>; Wong et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib71\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib71\">2017</a>), and the solutions from lensed AGNs should be directly portable to glSNe\u00a0Ia. Inferring the lens potential may even be easier than for AGNs as a more detailed reconstruction of the lensed SN\u00a0Ia host should be possible once the supernova has faded. glSNe\u00a0Ia with time-delays greater than a month are therefore golden lenses with which to measure <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>. With a method for extracting precise time delays from these objects in hand, a renewed focus should be placed on the discovery and follow up of glSNe\u00a0Ia.</p></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>D.A.G. gratefully acknowledges Eric Linder for a careful reading of the manuscript and for thoughtful feedback. Greg Aldering and Alex Kim also deserve thanks for feedback that improved the paper. D.A.G., T.E.C., and P.E.N. are members of the Large Synoptic Survey Telescope Dark Energy Science Collaboration (LSST-DESC), and they acknowledge Phil Marshall and the Strong Lensing working group for bringing them together so that this work could be launched. D.A.G. thanks Kyle Barbary for leading the development of <tt>sncosmo</tt> (Barbary et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib3\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib3\">2016</a>), which was essential to the analysis. The authors acknowledge support from the DOE under grant DE-AC02-05CH11231, Analytical Modeling for Extreme-Scale Computing Environments. D.N.K is supported in part by the U.S. Department of Energy, Office of Science, Office of Nuclear Physics, under contract number DE-AC02-05CH11231 and DE-SC0017616, and by a SciDAC award DE-SC0018297. This research was supported by the Gordon and Betty Moore Foundation through Grant GBMF5076. The authors are grateful to the Royal Society for an International Exchange Grant (IE170307), which has supported our collaboration on this work. This research used resources of the National Energy Research Scientific Computing Center, a DOE Office of Science User Facility supported by the Office of Science of the U.S. Department of Energy under Contract No. DE-AC02-05CH11231 as well as the <tt>savio</tt> cluster at UC Berkeley. Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f2\">2</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f9\">9</a>, and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f11\">11</a> were rendered with code based on <tt>ChainConsumer</tt> (Hinton <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib19\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib19\">2016</a>). This research has made use of NASA's Astrophysics Data System. This research made use of Astropy, a community-developed core Python package for Astronomy (Astropy Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib1\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib1\">2013</a>).</p></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaaa975app1\" name=\"apjaaa975app1\">Appendix A: Radiation Transport Simulation</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In this appendix, we provide the details of the radiation transport simulations that we use to calculate the time-evolving SN\u00a0Ia SED in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975s2-3\">2.3</a>. The <tt>SEDONA</tt> code (Kasen et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib25\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib25\">2006</a>) is a time-dependent, multi-dimensional Monte Carlo radiative transfer code, designed to calculate the light curves, spectra, and polarization of supernova explosion models. Given a homologously expanding SN ejecta structure, <tt>SEDONA</tt> calculates the full time series of emergent spectra at high wavelength resolution. In the present calculations, we employ a modified version of <tt>SEDONA</tt> that tags photons with their <em>P</em> and <em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phgr.gif\" alt=\"phgr\" align=\"absmiddle\" /></em> values, thus we calculate <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn58.gif\" alt=\"${I}_{\\lambda }(P,\\phi ,t,\\lambda )$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{I}_{\\lambda }(P,\\phi ,t,\\lambda )</script></span></span>. In addition to being fully time-dependent, the <tt>SEDONA</tt> calculation accounts for the extendedness of continuum emitting regions in SN\u00a0Ia atmospheres and the wavelength dependence of their location and extent. It also explicitly accounts for light travel time across the atmosphere. Broadband light curves are constructed by convolving the synthetic spectrum at each time with the appropriate instrumental throughputs. <tt>SEDONA</tt> includes a detailed treatment of gamma-ray transfer to determine the instantaneous energy deposition rate from radioactive <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn59.gif\" alt=\"${}^{56}\\mathrm{Ni}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{}^{56}\\mathrm{Ni}</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn60.gif\" alt=\"${}^{56}\\mathrm{Co}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{}^{56}\\mathrm{Co}</script></span></span> decay. Other decay chains that can change the composition of the ejecta, such as <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn61.gif\" alt=\"${}^{48}\\mathrm{Cr}\\to {}^{48}{\\rm{V}}\\to {}^{48}\\mathrm{Ti}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{}^{48}\\mathrm{Cr}\\to {}^{48}{\\rm{V}}\\to {}^{48}\\mathrm{Ti}</script></span></span>, are not treated. Radiative heating and cooling rates are evaluated from Monte Carlo estimators, and the temperature structure of the ejecta is determined by iterating the model to thermal equilibrium.</p><p>Several significant approximations are made in our <tt>SEDONA</tt> simulation, notably the assumption of local thermodynamic equilibrium (LTE) in computing the atomic level populations. In addition, bound\u2013bound line transitions are treated using the expansion opacity formalism (implying the Sobolev approximation; Jeffery <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib23\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib23\">1995</a>). In this formalism, the opacities of spectral lines within a wavelength bin are represented in aggregate by a single effective opacity. Although the <tt>SEDONA</tt> code is capable of a direct Monte Carlo treatment of NLTE line processes, due to computational constraints this functionality is not exploited here. Instead, the line source functions are treated using an approximate two-level atom approach. In the present calculations, we assume for simplicity that all lines are \"purely absorptive,\" i.e., in the two-level atom formalism the ratio of the probability of redistribution to pure scattering is taken to be <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn62.gif\" alt=\"${\\epsilon }_{\\mathrm{th}}=1$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\epsilon }_{\\mathrm{th}}=1</script></span></span> for all lines. In this case, the line source functions are given by the Planck function, consistent with our adoption of LTE level populations.</p><p>The numerical gridding in the present calculations was as follows: <em>spatial:</em> 100 equally spaced radial zones with a maximum velocity of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn63.gif\" alt=\"$4\\times {10}^{4}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">4\\times {10}^{4}</script></span></span> <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn64.gif\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}</script></span></span>; <em>temporal:</em> 459 time points beginning at day\u00a01 and extending to day 100 with logarithmic spacing <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn65.gif\" alt=\"${\\rm{\\Delta }}\\ \\mathrm{log}\\,t=0.175;$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\rm{\\Delta }}\\ \\mathrm{log}\\,t=0.175;</script></span></span> <em>wavelength:</em> covering the range 100\u201330,000\u00a0\u00c5 with a resolution of 10\u00a0\u00c5. Extensive testing confirms the adequacy of this griding for the problem at hand. Atomic line list data were taken from the Kurucz CD\u00a023 line list (Kurucz &amp; Bell <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib32\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib32\">1995</a>), which contains nearly 500,000 lines. 10<sup>10</sup> photon packets were used for the calculation, which allowed for acceptable signal-to-noise in the synthetic broadband light curves, spectra, and velocity-dependent specific intensity profiles.</p></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaaa975app2\" name=\"apjaaa975app2\">Appendix B: Derivation of Equation (9)</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The observed monochromatic flux density <em>F</em><sub><em>\u03bb</em></sub> of a source is obtained by setting up a small element of area <em>dA</em> perpendicular to the line of sight at the location of the observer, and integrating the specific intensity of the field <em>I</em><sub><em>\u03bb</em></sub> in the direction normal to <em>dA</em> over the solid angle subtended by the source (Rybicki &amp; Lightman <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib54\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib54\">1979</a>),</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn15\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn15.gif\" alt=\"Equation (15)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{F}_{\\lambda }=\\int {I}_{\\lambda }\\cos \\theta d{\\rm{\\Omega }}.\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t15\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>In Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975eqn15\">15</a>), <em>\u03b8</em> is defined by <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn66.gif\" alt=\"$\\tan \\theta =P/{D}_{L}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\tan \\theta =P/{D}_{L}</script></span></span>, where <em>D</em><sub><em>L</em></sub> is the luminosity distance from the observer to the closest point on the plane.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975fn12\">17</a></sup>\n From this definition, we can construct the radial differential <em>dP</em>,</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn16\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn16.gif\" alt=\"Equation (16)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{dP}={D}_{L}{\\sec }^{2}\\theta \\,d\\theta .\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t16\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>Using <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn67.gif\" alt=\"$d{\\rm{\\Omega }}\\equiv \\sin \\theta \\,d\\theta \\,d\\phi $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">d{\\rm{\\Omega }}\\equiv \\sin \\theta \\,d\\theta \\,d\\phi</script></span></span>, Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975eqn15\">15</a>) becomes</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn17\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn17.gif\" alt=\"Equation (17)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{F}_{\\lambda }={\\int }_{0}^{2\\pi }{\\int }_{0}^{{\\theta }_{m}}{I}_{\\lambda }\\cos \\theta \\sin \\theta \\,d\\theta \\,d\\phi ,\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t17\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn68.gif\" alt=\"${\\theta }_{m}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\theta }_{m}</script></span></span> is the maximum angular extent of the atmosphere. Making the change of variables shown in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975eqn16\">16</a>), and using <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn69.gif\" alt=\"$\\cos \\theta ={D}_{L}/\\sqrt{{P}^{2}+{D}_{L}^{2}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\cos \\theta ={D}_{L}/\\sqrt{{P}^{2}+{D}_{L}^{2}}</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn70.gif\" alt=\"$\\sin \\theta =P/\\sqrt{{P}^{2}+{D}_{L}^{2}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\sin \\theta =P/\\sqrt{{P}^{2}+{D}_{L}^{2}}</script></span></span>, Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975eqn17\">17</a>) becomes</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn18\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn18.gif\" alt=\"Equation (18)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{F}_{\\lambda }={\\int }_{0}^{2\\pi }{\\int }_{0}^{{P}_{m}}\\displaystyle \\frac{{{PD}}_{L}^{2}{I}_{\\lambda }}{{({P}^{2}+{D}_{L}^{2})}^{2}}\\,{dP}\\,d\\phi .\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t18\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>As we are in the <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn71.gif\" alt=\"$P\\ll {D}_{L}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">P\\ll {D}_{L}</script></span></span> limit, by Taylor expanding the denominator of the integrand of Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975eqn18\">18</a>) in powers of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn72.gif\" alt=\"$(P/{D}_{L})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">(P/{D}_{L})</script></span></span> and keeping only first-order terms, Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975eqn18\">18</a>) reduces to</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaaa975eqn19\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975eqn19.gif\" alt=\"Equation (19)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{F}_{\\lambda }={D}_{L}^{-2}{\\int }_{0}^{2\\pi }{\\int }_{0}^{{P}_{m}}{I}_{\\lambda }\\,P\\,{dP}\\,d\\phi .\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t19\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>Since lensing conserves surface brightness, the application of a spatially varying microlensing magnification pattern transforms <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn73.gif\" alt=\"${I}_{\\lambda }\\to \\mu (P,\\phi ){I}_{\\lambda }(P,\\phi )$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{I}_{\\lambda }\\to \\mu (P,\\phi ){I}_{\\lambda }(P,\\phi )</script></span></span>. Making this substitution in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975eqn19\">19</a>), we are left with Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaaa975eqn9\">9</a>). A schematic of the integration geometry is presented in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaa975f13\">13</a>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaa975f13\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaa975f13\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f13_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 13.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 13.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975f13_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 13.</strong>\u00a0Integration geometry for Appendix <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaa975app2\">B</a>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaa975f13\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaa975f13\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaa975f13\" href=\"/0004-637X/855/1/22/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaa975f13\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div></div><!-- End article full text -->\n<!-- Start Footnotes -->\n\t<h2 id=\"footnotes\">Footnotes</h2>\n\t\t<div data-mobile-collapse=\"\">\n\t\t\t<ul class=\"clear-list wd-content-footnotes\">\n\t\t\t\t<li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaaa975fn1\" id=\"apjaaa975fn1\">6\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>N.B. The general relativistic time delays introduced by microlensing into macroimages are of the order of microseconds (Moore &amp; Hewitt <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib40\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib40\">1996</a>)\u2014too small to detect. The uncertainty that microlensing introduces into time delays is solely due to time- and wavelength-dependent distortions of macroimage light curves and spectra.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaaa975fn2\" id=\"apjaaa975fn2\">7\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>This box size was chosen to be large enough to accommodate the effects of ellipticity and external shear.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaaa975fn3\" id=\"apjaaa975fn3\">8\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The remainder of the convergence is assumed to take the form of continuously distributed matter (i.e., dark matter).</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaaa975fn4\" id=\"apjaaa975fn4\">9\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>Spectropolarimetry indicates that SNe\u00a0Ia are globally spherically symmetric to ~a few percent (see Wang &amp; Wheeler <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaa975bib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaaa975bib69\">2008</a>).</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaaa975fn5\" id=\"apjaaa975fn5\">10\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>In this paper, <em>\u03bc</em> refers exclusively to lensing amplification. Nowhere should <em>\u03bc</em> be interpreted as <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/855/1/22/Full/apjaaa975ieqn39.gif\" alt=\"$\\mu =\\cos \\theta $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\mu =\\cos \\theta</script></span></span>, the viewing angle parameter that frequently appears in supernova modeling papers.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaaa975fn6\" id=\"apjaaa975fn6\">11\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>We refer here to cosmological redshift only; Doppler shifts due to supernova expansion velocity are accounted for implicitly in the radiation transport simulation.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaaa975fn7\" id=\"apjaaa975fn7\">12\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>As our model is spherically symmetric, <em>I</em><sub><em>\u03bb</em></sub> possesses no <em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phgr.gif\" alt=\"phgr\" align=\"absmiddle\" /></em>-dependence.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaaa975fn8\" id=\"apjaaa975fn8\">13\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>\n<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"https://github.com/LSSTScienceCollaborations/ObservingStrategy\">https://github.com/LSSTScienceCollaborations/ObservingStrategy</a>\n</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaaa975fn9\" id=\"apjaaa975fn9\">14\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The Hsiao template is an empirical, time-dependent SED model constructed from the observed spectra of many SNe\u00a0Ia but it does not contain position dependent information (i.e., <em>P</em>, <em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phgr.gif\" alt=\"phgr\" align=\"absmiddle\" /></em>), so to calculate <em>\u03bc</em>(<em>\u03bb</em>, <em>t</em>) theoretical models are needed. The Hsiao template is more accurate than the theoretical model in the IR and after maximum light when NLTE effects become important.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaaa975fn10\" id=\"apjaaa975fn10\">15\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>N.B. If \u0394<em>t</em> is distributed as a Gaussian with width <em>\u03c3</em>, then the ensemble average of <em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/epsi.gif\" alt=\"epsilon\" align=\"baseline\" /></em> is only 0.79<em>\u03c3</em>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaaa975fn11\" id=\"apjaaa975fn11\">16\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>A full description of the observations is available at <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"https://archive.stsci.edu/proposal_search.php?mission=hst&amp;id=14862\">https://archive.stsci.edu/proposal_search.php?mission=hst&amp;id=14862</a>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaaa975fn12\" id=\"apjaaa975fn12\">17\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The luminosity distance (not the angular diameter distance) is used here because the intrinsic luminosity of the source is known.</p></p></div></li></ul>\n\t\t</div>\n\t<!-- End Footnotes -->\t\n<!-- Start References List -->\n\n<div Computational Cosmology Center, Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory, 1 Cyclotron Road, Berkeley, CA 94720, USA;), AB(Department of Astronomy, University of California, Berkeley, CA 94720, USA ; Computational Cosmology Center, Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory, 1 Cyclotron Road, Berkeley, CA 94720, USA), AC(Department of Astronomy, University of California, Berkeley, CA 94720, USA ;  Nuclear Science Division, Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory, 1 Cyclotron Road, Berkeley, CA 94720, USA ;  Department of Physics, University of California, Berkeley, CA 94720, USA), AD(Institute of Cosmology and Gravitation, University of Portsmouth, Dennis Sciama Building, Burnaby Road, Portsmouth, PO1 3FX, UK)"], "journal_name": "The Astrophysical Journal", "volume": "855", "publication_year": "2018/03/1", "start_page": "22", "keywords": ["gravitational lensing: micro", "gravitational lensing: strong", "Supernovae: general", "Astrophysics - Cosmology and Nongalactic Astrophysics"], "url": "https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2018ApJ...855...22G", "abstract": "Time delays between the multiple images of strongly gravitationally lensed Type Ia supernovae (glSNe Ia) have the potential to deliver precise cosmological constraints, but the effects of microlensing on time delay extraction have not been studied in detail. Here we quantify the effect of microlensing on the glSN Ia yield of the Large Synoptic Survey Telescope (LSST) and the effect of microlensing on the precision and accuracy of time delays that can be extracted from LSST glSNe Ia. Microlensing has a negligible effect on the LSST glSN Ia yield, but it can be increased by a factor of \u223c2 over previous predictions to 930 systems using a novel photometric identification technique based on spectral template fitting. Crucially, the microlensing of glSNe Ia is achromatic until three rest-frame weeks after the explosion, making the early-time color curves microlensing-insensitive time delay indicators. By fitting simulated flux and color observations of microlensed glSNe Ia with their underlying, unlensed spectral templates, we forecast the distribution of absolute time delay error due to microlensing for LSST, which is unbiased at the sub-percent level and peaked at 1% for color curve observations in the achromatic phase, while for light-curve observations it is comparable to state-of-the-art mass modeling uncertainties (4%). About 70% of LSST glSN Ia images should be discovered during the achromatic phase, indicating that microlensing time delay uncertainties can be minimized if prompt multicolor follow-up observations are obtained. Accounting for microlensing, the 1-2 day time delay on the recently discovered glSN Ia iPTF16geu can be measured to 40% precision, limiting its cosmological utility.", "doi": "10.3847/1538-4357/aaa975", "custom1": "eprint: arXiv:1708.00003", "issn": "0004-637X", "number": "1"}, "Inspection status": "Complete"}
